                    <td>5/13/2014</td><td class="td-badge"><div class="rg badge">RG</div></td><td><a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/fantasy/subsisting-carlos-ruiz-kurt-suzuki-ryan-hanigan-john-jaso/">Catcher Subsisting with Ruiz, Suzuki, Hanigan, Jas&#8230;</a><span class="m_author"> by <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/fantasy/index.php?author=44136">Nicholas Minnix</a></span></td></tr><tr>

        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>Hanigan had roughly the season one might expect of him given his recent minor league numbers. Regard: in 1,291 Double- and Triple-A plate appearances, Hanigan hit 12 homers (0.9% HR/PA), walked 156 times, and struck out 151 times (1.03 BB/K). In 2009, over 293 PAs, Hanigan hit three homers (1.0% HR/PA), walked unintentionally 37 times, and struck out 31 times (1.19 BB/K). In fact, Hanigan ranked ninth in BB/K ratio among players with 250+ PAs. The eight guys ahead of him are indicative of the sort of players who typically occupy such a list – that is, the Crazy Good (Pujols, Pedroia, Helton, and Mauer) and the Small or Scrappy (Luis Rodruiguez, Yadier Molina, Carlos Ruiz, and Marco Scutaro). Hanigan is firmly ensconced in the latter group.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Hanigan enters 2010 much the same way he did 2009: with Ramon Hernandez ahead of him on the depth chart. The Reds declined the $8.5 million option on the latter's contract but signed him soon after to a $3 million deal. The deal's a bit of a mystery: in 2009, Hanigan was roughly the same offensive player as Hernandez in roughly the same number of PAs, totaling -4.7 wRAA to Hernandez's -4.6. Is Hernandez's defense significantly better? Is this a case of Dusty Baker et al. just liking the veteran presence? Maybe and probably. Either way, it seems like a lot of money to spend on a marginal improvement. Even during his best days, from the beginning of May to the end of June, Hanigan was still a negligible fantasy contributor, rating just inside in the top-25 ML catchers over that stretch.  (Carson Cistulli)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Although the Reds are bringing back veteran Ramon Hernandez for one last season, Hanigan likely hit his way into an extended role with the team in 2010. He showed some power (.128 ISO) for the first time in his career, and he parlayed that in to a .300/.405/.429 line in 243 plate appearances as Hernandez's backup. This power boost just serves to build on excellent plate skills, as Hanigan walks at a high rate (12.6% career) and strikes out at a low rate (11.5% career). That combination is what give Hanigan the potential to put up .400 on base percentages, even with only marginally high BABIPs like his .313 last season. Hanigan's fantasy value is going to be highly dependent on his playing time. Due to Hernandez's age, it wouldn't be a shock to see Hanigan pick up a majority of the catcher PAs, and his average and the potent Reds lineup should make him a decent play in NL-only leagues. (Jack Moore)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Hanigan seems primed for a big year at the catching position. However, it won't matter if the Reds stick with Ramon Hernandez as the starting catcher.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>One might think that Ramon Hernandez’s depature for Colorado would make both him and former platoon-mate Hanigan better fantasy options, since they won’t be splitting time anymore. Unfortunately for Hanigan, he loses a partner just in time to gain a protege. 23-year-old Devin Mesoraco got 13 starts in the month of September as the Reds tried to establish how soon he’ll be ready to take over the starting job. Hanigan should start the year off as the Reds’ catcher, but he can’t be blamed for looking over his shoulder; Mesoraco is the future, and the Reds hope that future comes sooner rather than later. Hanigan may be a stable play for the first month of the season, but once Mesoraco establishes himself, Hanigan’s share of the time will drop drastically. (Dan Wade)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>If a star catcher goes down early, Hanigan could be a decent candidate to cover that gap, but the presence of Mesoraco makes it unlikely that he’ll be on sure ground all year. Anyone in need of a full season starter should look elsewhere. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Hanigan is the type of player that frustrates fantasy owners. He is a solid two-to-three win catcher, yet he provides negligible value in standard rotisserie leagues. Power is non-existent with only two home runs last season, he has never stolen a base in his big league career, and his career-high RBI total is a mere 40, which came back in 2010. He does possess a 12.1% career walk rate -- so he can provide value in one category for those leagues that include on-base percentage -- but that’s nothing that will excite fantasy owners. Not to mention top prospect Devin Mesoraco still looms in the shadows, poised to steal significant playing time if his bat comes around. In terms of fantasy value, he ranks in the bottom third of the league. He should not be on your team unless you’re absolutely desperate. You’re looking at a John Jaso kind of player with less power. No thank you. (JP Breen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Hanigan should retain the starting job in Cincinnati due to his plus defense and on-base skills, but neither of those attributes make the 32-year-old particularly valuable in standard fantasy formats. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>It was one thing for the Reds to replace Ryan Hanigan after his miserable, injury-filled 2013. It was another thing to do it with Brayan Pena, a likable fellow who is also clearly a replacement-level catcher. The Rays picked up Hanigan and while he is an asset, his shiny new three-year contract was something of a surprise. Hanigan is good defensively, especially when it comes to pitch framing, but that does not do fantasy owners much good. At the plate, what little offense he does generate is entirely due to a high rate of contact and frequent walks. The walks will probably come down at least a bit now that he won't be hitting in front of the pitcher, but even without that consideration, Hanigan is not much use in fantasy. He's probably a .250/.340/.340 hitter. He might be acceptable as a second catcher in deep AL-only leagues, but that is about it. (Matt Klaassen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ryan Hanigan was a nice pickup by the Rays, but unless your fantasy league includes catcher defense, he does not merit consideration outside of the deepest AL-only leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Hanigan is an interesting player for some of us. (Okay, maybe just for me.) He combines excellent plate discipline and contact ability with a great defensive reputation that’s backed up by metrics (such as they are). As a fantasy owner, you shouldn’t care about any of that. Even by catcher standards, he has never contributed significantly in any of the counting stats, and even though he figures –– if he can stay healthy –– to be Boston's primary backup catcher in 2015, his production doesn’t figure to change. In deeper two-catcher leagues or some AL-only leagues, he might contribute in batting average if he goes through a period of good luck with batted balls; the trouble is that you never know when that’s going to happen. Hanigan has shown some ability to hit opposite handed pitching in the past, but even those days seem to be over. In daily leagues, you’d be better off platooning someone like Rene Rivera. (<a href="https://twitter.com/RobertJBaumann">Robert J. Baumann</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Hanigan has never really played enough –– nor hit enough when he’s played –– to be a fantasy contributor. It’s unlikely either of those things will change as he enters his age-34 season.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>There was a time when I thought Ryan Hanigan could be a good starting catcher. I'm pretty sure I was right even though he never really got the chance. Hanigan is one of the top pitch framers in the league on a per game basis. Unfortunately, his prime came before pitch framing was a widely discussed topic. He was viewed as a high contact, no power hitter. In more recent seasons, his strikeout rate has increased above his walk rate. Last year was arguably his worst at the plate -- not a big surprise for a 35-year-old catcher. His non-trivial platoon splits include an elite OBP versus left-handed pitching. He still doesn't offer much in the way of power. The Red Sox will use him to ease the burden on Blake Swihart as he adjusts to the majors, but he may lose his spot on the roster to Christian Vazquez. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Hanigan is a pitch framing and platoon walking specialist. He can help the Red Sox command and control pitchers to get the most of their outings while also racking up free passes against left-handed pitching. At this point in his career, his most important job is mentoring Swihart.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Once upon a time, Ryan Hanigan was good at hitting. He posted a 126 wRC+ in 2010, for instance, making him 26% more productive than the league-adjusted average. Then his performance slipped demonstrably, down to 100, then 88, then 52. He managed to rebound in 2014 and 2015, but then really backslid to an unfathomable 20 wRC+ in 2016, the third-worst mark in baseball (min. 100 plate appearances). Unfortunately for the good people of New England, his manager was the last person to notice, as he allowed him to start games all the way into September. Mercifully, his contract ended, and now the manager can't play him anymore, and the good people of New England will live happily ever after. The end.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>One time, Ryan Hanigan hit six home runs all in the same season. That was in 2011. In a related story, Ryan Hanigan might be done as a major league catcher.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>

            <td align="left" style="width: 175px">
